Australia</fundingAgency><fundingNumber>2020</fundingNumber></fundingInfo><abstractGroup><abstract type="main" xml:lang="en"><title type="main">Abstract</title><p>Rechargeable sodium‐ion batteries (SIBs), as the most promising alternative to commercial lithium‐ion batteries, have received tremendous attention during the last decade. Among all the anode materials for SIBs, metal sulfides/selenides (MXs) have shown inspiring results because of their versatile material species and high theoretical capacity. They suffer from large volume expansion, however, which leads to bad cycling performance. Thus, methods such as carbon modification, nanosize design, electrolyte optimization, and cut‐off voltage control are used to obtain enhanced performance. Here, recent progress on MXs is summarized in terms of arranging the crystal structure, synthesis methods, electrochemical performance, mechanisms, and kinetics. Challenges are presented and effective ways to solve the problems are proposed, and a perspective for future material design is also given. It is hoped that light is shed on the development of MXs to help finally find applications for next‐generation rechargeable batteries.</p></abstract><abstract type="graphical" xml:lang="en" xml:id="adma201700606-abs-0001"><p><b>Metal sulfides and metal selenides</b> have shown great progress as anode materials for sodium‐ion batteries because of their versatile material species, easily controlled morphology, and high theoretical specific capacity. Their advances and challenges are summarized, showing their importance for next‐generation energy storage and conversion devices.<blockFixed type="graphic"><mediaResourceGroup><mediaResource href="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma201700606:adma201700606-gra-0001" alt="image"/><mediaResource mimeType="image/png" href="graphic/adma201700606-gra-0001-m.png" rendition="webHiRes" alt="image"/></mediaResourceGroup></blockFixed></p></abstract></abstractGroup></contentMeta></header><body xml:id="adma201700606-body-0001" sectionsNumbered="yes"><section xml:id="adma201700606-sec-0010"><label role="interim">1</label><title type="main">Introduction</title><p>The ever‐growing demand for electrical‐energy‐storage (EES) devices is providing great opportunities and challenges. Especially for the rechargeable ion batteries, great success has been achieved during the last two decades.<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0001"/> Since the 1990s, the lithium‐ion batteries (LIBs) have been commercialized and dominated the market owing to their high safety, stability, and capability of storing and converting clean energy to provide a constant power supply. Due to limited lithium resources, however, it is desirable to find an alternative source for next generation EES devices.<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0002"/> Recently, sodium‐ion batteries (SIBs) have emerged as one of the most promising candidates because sodium‐containing compounds are highly abundant and have cheap raw materials (<b>Figure</b> <link href="#adma201700606-fig-0001"><b>1</b></link>a).<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0003 #adma201700606-bib-0004"/> Compared to the Li‐ion‐battery system, sodium possesses a larger ionic radius (1.02 Å for Na<sup>+</sup> vs 0.76 Å for Li<sup>+</sup>) and a reduction potential about 300 mV higher than lithium. In lithium‐containing compounds, lithium can exist in octahedral or tetrahedral coordination. Sodium rarely has the tetrahedral coordination, but prefers octahedral and prismatic coordination. In general, these differences may cause a sluggish reaction mechanism and critical conditions for sodium‐ion diffusion, which means that some materials may not be suitable for SIBs. For example, graphite displays a very low capacity (lower than 35 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>) in SIBs.<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0005 #adma201700606-bib-0006"/> Thus, it is urgent to find good anode materials for SIBs in the near future.</p><figure xml:id="adma201700606-fig-0001"><mediaResourceGroup><mediaResource href="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma201700606:adma201700606-fig-0001" alt="image"/><mediaResource mimeType="image/png" href="graphic/adma201700606-fig-0001-m.png" rendition="webHiRes" alt="image"/></mediaResourceGroup><caption>a) Schematic illustration of room‐temperature SIBs. b) Reaction mechanisms of anode materials for LIBs. Reproduced with permission.<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0007"/> Copyright 2009, Royal Society of Chemistry.</caption></figure><p>The reaction mechanisms of anode materials for SIBs are similar to those for LIBs, which can always be classified into three categories: intercalation, conversion, and alloying (Figure <link href="#adma201700606-fig-0001"/>b).<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0007"/> The materials with intercalation reactions have obvious charge and discharge platforms and feature a relatively low theoretical capacity.<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0008 #adma201700606-bib-0009 #adma201700606-bib-0010"/> Conversion reactions provide high theoretical capacity, always above 400 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>, due to multiple‐electron reactions during cycling. The conversion reaction suffers, however, from large volume changes and voltage polarization.<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0011 #adma201700606-bib-0012 #adma201700606-bib-0013 #adma201700606-bib-0014"/> Alloying type materials, such as red phosphorus, tin, and antimony, also have high theoretical capacity, and the reaction is also accompanied by large volume expansion.<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0015 #adma201700606-bib-0016 #adma201700606-bib-0017 #adma201700606-bib-0018"/> Typical anode materials include carbon materials, metal phosphides, metal oxides, metal sulfides, metal selenides, sodium‐containing compounds, etc.<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0009 #adma201700606-bib-0016 #adma201700606-bib-0019 #adma201700606-bib-0020 #adma201700606-bib-0021 #adma201700606-bib-0022 #adma201700606-bib-0023"/> In terms of the carbon materials, as mentioned above, graphite has a very low practical capacity in carbon‐ester electrolyte. Hard carbon could be a good choice because of the stable cycling performance.<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0024 #adma201700606-bib-0025 #adma201700606-bib-0026"/> The synthesis of hard carbon needs high‐temperature treatment (above 1000 °C), however, and safety problems such as sodium dendrites may be caused by the low reaction voltage range (below 0.1 V). Graphene and porous carbon materials are also proved to be electrochemically active in SIBs, but the low Coulombic efficiency in the first cycle, undesirable side reactions derived from the large surface area, and the high cost means that they could not meet the requirements for practical use.<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0027 #adma201700606-bib-0028 #adma201700606-bib-0029"/> Metal phosphides have recently received great attention because of their high theoretical capacity. Their synthesis methods, however, are limited because their raw material is dangerous red phosphorus.<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0016"/> Metal oxides have been extensively investigated in LIBs, and excellent long‐term cycling performance can be achieved by carbon modification and suitable morphology design.<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0021 #adma201700606-bib-0022"/> On the contrary, FeO, CoO, and NiO show almost no electrochemical activity in SIBs.<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0030"/></p><p>Metal sulfides/selenides (MXs) have been investigated as electrode materials for LIBs since the 1970s, such as titanium disulfide, which can achieve very high capacity and long cycling life according to laboratory tests.<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0031 #adma201700606-bib-0032"/> They are also very popular in SIBs because of their easily‐controlled morphology and high theoretical specific capacity, which makes them very competitive with other anode materials. Numerous excellent reviews focused on the general conversion reaction for SIBs have given a brief introduction to MXs.<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0033 #adma201700606-bib-0034 #adma201700606-bib-0035 #adma201700606-bib-0036 #adma201700606-bib-0037 #adma201700606-bib-0038 #adma201700606-bib-0039 #adma201700606-bib-0040 #adma201700606-bib-0041"/> Here, recent progress on MXs for SIBs is systematically summarized. The synthesis methods, electrochemical performances, reaction mechanisms, and kinetics of both the layered and nonlayered MXs are introduced in detail. Their challenges, improvement strategies, and outlook are also discussed. In the future, more research needs to focus on the conversion reaction to bring conversion type materials to commercialization.</p></section><section xml:id="adma201700606-sec-0020"><label role="interim">2</label><title type="main">Theories and Challenges for MXs</title><section xml:id="adma201700606-sec-0030"><label role="interim">2.1</label><title type="main">Theories</title><section xml:id="adma201700606-sec-0040"><label role="interim">2.1.1</label><title type="main">Platform Changes</title><p>Platform changes during cycling are very common in MXs, especially in the first two cycles. According to previous reports, some MXs cannot be reproduced after the initial discharge process.<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0042 #adma201700606-bib-0043"/> For example, 2H‐MoS<sub>2</sub> was proved to be irreversible during cycling by in situ X‐ray diffraction (XRD) characterization.<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0042"/> The charging product was 1T‐MoS<sub>2</sub> in the case where no more than 1.5 Na<sup>+</sup> was intercalated into the MoS<sub>2</sub> in the initial sodiation process. When more than 1.5 Na<sup>+</sup> was reacted with MoS<sub>2</sub>, Mo nanocrystallites and sodium sulfides were detected, and in the charging process, the MoS<sub>2</sub> was never reproduced. Platform changes always have a bad influence on the cycling performance and Coulombic efficiency.</p></section><section xml:id="adma201700606-sec-0050"><label role="interim">2.1.2</label><title type="main">Sodium Polysulfides</title><p>As sodium sulfides are considered to be the most common discharge products of the conversion reaction between metal sulfides and sodium, polysulfides should be a possible intermediate, which will react with carbon‐ester‐based electrolyte and dissolve into ether‐based or carbon‐ester‐based electrolyte, leading to severe capacity loss and safety issues.<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0044 #adma201700606-bib-0045"/></p></section><section xml:id="adma201700606-sec-0060"><label role="interim">2.1.3</label><title type="main">Sodium Ion Diffusion</title><p>Nanostructured materials have a large surface area and numerous active sites for sodium‐ion diffusion and storage, which guarantees fast reaction kinetics resulting in high capacity and good rate capability.<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0046 #adma201700606-bib-0047 #adma201700606-bib-0048 #adma201700606-bib-0049"/> The ionic diffusion coefficient (<i>D</i><sub>ion</sub>), diffusion length (<i>l</i>), and diffusion time (τ) usually have the following relationship <displayedItem type="mathematics" numbered="yes" xml:id="adma201700606-disp-0001"><mediaResource href="graphic/adma201700606-math-0001.png" alt="urn:x-wiley:dummy:media:adma201700606:adma201700606-math-0001"/></displayedItem></p><p>For the same materials with same structural parameters, the <i>D</i><sub>ion</sub> is always a constant. Thus τ is proportional to <i>l</i><sup>2</sup>. Therefore nanosized structure can shorten the diffusion time of sodium ions and achieve high rate capability.</p></section><section xml:id="adma201700606-sec-0070"><label role="interim">2.1.4</label><title type="main">Pseudocapacitance</title><p>According to density functional theory (DFT) calculations, some nanosized sodium metal sulfides feature metallic conductivity,<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0050"/> which makes a capacitance contribution to the battery system. The charge and discharge processes include three kinds of capacity contribution: the faradaic contribution from the sodium intercalation process, the faradaic contribution from the charge transfer caused by incorporation of sodium ions in the material's surface, expressed as pseudocapacitance, and the non‐Faradaic contribution from double layer capacitance.<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0045 #adma201700606-bib-0051 #adma201700606-bib-0052 #adma201700606-bib-0053 #adma201700606-bib-0054 #adma201700606-bib-0055 #adma201700606-bib-0056 #adma201700606-bib-0057"/> From the cyclic voltammogram (CV) data, the measured current (<i>i</i>) at fixed voltage (<i>U</i>) and the scan rate (ν) can be related by Equation <link href="#adma201700606-disp-0002"/>, where <i>k</i><sub>1</sub>ν and <i>k</i><sub>2</sub>ν<sup>1/2</sup> represent the current contributions from surface capacitance and the redox reaction. By analyzing the CV data at different scan rates and neglecting the slight shifts for the specific voltages, the values of the parameters <i>k</i><sub>1</sub> and <i>k</i><sub>2</sub> can be calculated. Thus, pseudocapacitance during the charge and discharge processes is able to be quantified. When the size of active materials decreases to the nanosize, the pseudocapacitance can make a significant contribution to the charge and discharge processes, which indicates fast Na<sup>+</sup> migration and good rate performance<displayedItem type="mathematics" numbered="yes" xml:id="adma201700606-disp-0002"><mediaResource href="graphic/adma201700606-math-0002.png" alt="urn:x-wiley:dummy:media:adma201700606:adma201700606-math-0002"/></displayedItem></p></section></section><section xml:id="adma201700606-sec-0080"><label role="interim">2.2</label><title type="main">Challenges and Methods</title><p>Although MXs have many advantages, there are still several problems. First, the final discharge products may have side reactions with the electrolyte. Second, large volume expansion is caused by the large amount of sodium participating in the electrochemical reaction. Third, the solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) film leads to large irreversible capacity in the first cycle. Fourth, the low conductivity and ion‐diffusion coefficient degrade the rate performance. The most common strategies to solve these problems are nanostructure design, carbon modification, electrolyte optimization, and voltage range selection. Recent progress has led to great improvements in MXs, and the synthesis methods and electrochemical performances have been summarized in <b>Table</b> <link href="#adma201700606-tbl-0001"><b>1</b></link>.</p><tabular xml:id="adma201700606-tbl-0001" pRights="unknown" eRights="yes" copyright="John Wiley &amp; Sons, Ltd."><title type="main">Recent progress xml:id="adma201700606-tbl1-note-0001"><label><sup>a)</sup></label><p>The cycling data are summarized as capacity/corresponding cycle number/corresponding current density. The unit of capacity is mA h g<sup>−1</sup>. For all the data, 1C equals 1000 mA g<sup>−1</sup></p></note><note xml:id="adma201700606-tbl1-note-0002"><label><sup>b)</sup></label><p>The rate capability is summarized as capacity/corresponding current density. The unit of capacity is mA h g<sup>−1</sup>. For all the data, 1C equals 1000 mA g<sup>−1</sup>.</p></note></noteGroup></tabular><section xml:id="adma201700606-sec-0090"><label role="interim">2.2.1</label><title type="main">Structure Design</title><p>The general synthesis methods to prepare MXs include solvothermal reaction, spray pyrolysis, chemical vapor deposition (CVD), electrospinning, exfoliation, sulfuration or selenization, reflux, ball milling, etc. Some materials need to be further treated by freeze‐drying or high‐temperature calcination. As for the morphology design, nanosized particles can effectively minimize the effects of volume expansion and also provide a short ion‐diffusion length. Yolk–shell‐structured and hollow‐structured materials have been widely investigated to improve their performance.<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0058"/> Both of these structures introduce more contact area between the active materials and the electrolyte, provide more active sites, so as to enhance the electrochemical performances of the target materials.</p></section><section xml:id="adma201700606-sec-0100"><label role="interim">2.2.2</label><title type="main">Carbon Modification</title><p>Carbon materials are widely used as electrodes for rechargeable ion batteries because of their high electrical conductivity, high stability, and low cost. Introducing carbon analogs such as carbon nanoparticles, carbon nanotubes, and graphene into MXs materials can improve their electrical conductivity and stability to enhance their rate capability and cycling performance. In general, organic materials can be coated onto the material surface and be carbonized through high‐temperature treatment in an Ar or N<sub>2</sub> atmosphere. Some materials cannot be synthesized in this way, however, to obtain a carbon coating due to their inferior thermal stability. For example, FeS<sub>2</sub> will convert into FeS and S at temperatures above 500 °C, and CuS will lose S to form Cu<sub>2</sub>S. Therefore reports are mainly focused on the sulfuration or selenization of metal oxides/carbon to obtain the MXs/C materials. Meanwhile, introducing porous structures or doping with nitrogen or phosphorus can further enhance the stability of active materials through alleviating large volume expansion and increasing the electrical conductivity.</p></section><section xml:id="adma201700606-sec-0110"><label role="interim">2.2.3</label><title type="main">Electrolyte Optimization</title><p>Commonly used electrolytes can be mainly classified into carbon‐ester‐based electrolytes such as ethylene carbonate (EC), propylene carbonate (PC), diethyl carbonate (DEC), fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC), etc., ether‐based electrolytes, including dimethyl ether, diglyme, tetraglyme, etc., ionic liquids, and solid‐state electrolytes. The carbon‐ester‐based electrolytes will react with polysulfides and have bad effects on the cycling performance and the solid‐state electrolytes feature very low ionic conductivity. Thus, ether‐based electrolytes have been widely investigated to help achieve long‐life battery performance.<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0059 #adma201700606-bib-0060"/></p></section><section xml:id="adma201700606-sec-0120"><label role="interim">2.2.4</label><title type="main">Cut‐Off Voltage</title><p>Most MXs possess multiple reaction mechanism during the first discharge process. In general, the intercalation reaction will be the first to occur. Then, the conversion reaction takes place, accompanied by large volume expansion and irreversible capacity. Some reports have applied a higher terminal voltage to avoid the conversion reaction and obtain long cycling performance.<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0061 #adma201700606-bib-0062"/> For example, bare MoS<sub>2</sub> suffered a great capacity loss when cycled in the range of 0.1–3.0 V. It was different, however, when the terminal voltage was 0.4 V.<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0056"/> By controlling the amount of sodium intercalated, stable capacity can last for hundreds of cycles with no obvious fading.</p></section></section></section><section xml:id="adma201700606-sec-0130"><label role="interim">3</label><title type="main">Layered MXs</title><p>Layered MXs have recently attracted great attention because of their unique structure. Common layered MXs include MoS<sub>2</sub>, MoSe<sub>2</sub>, WS<sub>2</sub>, TiS<sub>2</sub>, VS<sub>2</sub>, and SnS<sub>2</sub>. Monolayer MXs have special physical properties such as good electrical conductivity (<b>Figure</b> <link href="#adma201700606-fig-0002"><b>2</b></link>a).<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0050"/> Two types of S–M coordination exist. One is trigonal prismatic (H‐phase, Figure <link href="#adma201700606-fig-0002"/>b), and the other is octahedral coordination (T‐phase, Figure <link href="#adma201700606-fig-0002"/>c). For bulk MXs, the weak van der Waals forces between each set of X–M–X layers are easy to break down, and then the alkali ions can insert themselves into the interlayers. With continuous sodium intercalation, the conversion reaction takes place. Metal and sodium chalcogenides are finally found in the fully discharged state. For some materials, the sodium sulfide or sodium selenide and metal cannot be recovered to the original layered material, which means that the expanded‐layer structure would have a limited effect on the electrochemical performance during the following cycles.</p><figure xml:id="adma201700606-fig-0002"><mediaResourceGroup><mediaResource href="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma201700606:adma201700606-fig-0002" alt="image"/><mediaResource mimeType="image/png" href="graphic/adma201700606-fig-0002-m.png" rendition="webHiRes" alt="image"/></mediaResourceGroup><caption>a) Comparison of T and H structural stability for 44 kinds of metal oxides and chalcogenides compounds from DFT calculations. Atomic monolayers from top and side views for the b) H phase and the c) T phase. Reproduced with permission.<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0050"/> Copyright 2012, American Chemistry Society.</caption></figure><section xml:id="adma201700606-sec-0140"><label role="interim">3.1</label><title type="main">Molybdenum and Tungsten Dichalcogenides</title><p>Molybdenum and tungsten dichalcogenides, MX<sub>2</sub> (M = Mo and W, X = S and Se), belong to the 2D transition‐metal dichalcogenide (TMD) group. The unique layered structure makes them suitable for wide applications as photocatalysts and solid lubricants, and in electrical‐energy‐storage devices.</p><section xml:id="adma201700606-sec-0150"><label role="interim">3.1.1</label><title type="main">MoS<sub>2</sub></title><p>MoS<sub>2</sub> is one of the most important members of the 2D TMDs group. The unique sandwiched S–Mo–S layers are connected by van der Waals forces with a typical layer distance of 0.62 nm. According to the different layer‐stacking sequences, MoS<sub>2</sub> can be divided into the two‐layer‐stacked hexagonal polymorph 2H‐MoS<sub>2</sub>, the one‐layer‐stacked trigonal 1T‐MoS<sub>2</sub>, and the three‐layer‐stacked rhombohedral 3R‐MoS<sub>2</sub>, as shown in <b>Figure</b> <link href="#adma201700606-fig-0003"><b>3</b></link>a–c. In each layer, Mo and S exhibit trigonal prismatic (2H‐MoS<sub>2</sub> and 3R‐MoS<sub>2</sub>) or octahedral coordination (1T‐MoS<sub>2</sub>). Among them, 2H‐MoS<sub>2</sub> is a room‐temperature stable product obtained by general synthesis methods, such as solvothermal reaction,<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0063 #adma201700606-bib-0064 #adma201700606-bib-0065 #adma201700606-bib-0066 #adma201700606-bib-0067 #adma201700606-bib-0068"/> microwave assisted solvothermal reaction,<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0069"/> spray pyrolysis,<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0070"/> CVD,<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0071"/> and electrospinning.<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0072"/> Wang et al. elucidated the mechanism by in situ XRD, ex situ XRD, and scanning transmission electron microscopy characterization of bulk MoS<sub>2</sub> samples (Figure <link href="#adma201700606-fig-0003"/>d–l).<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0042"/> When sodium ions are intercalated into the interlayers, the van der Waals forces are broken, leading to phase and stacking sequence changes. The initial discharge mechanism of MoS<sub>2</sub> in SIBs has been reported (Equations <link href="#adma201700606-disp-0003"/>–<link href="#adma201700606-disp-0005"/>). When the terminal discharge voltage was 0.2 V, no metal Mo nanoparticles and sodium sulfides were generated, and during the deintercalation process, a solid‐solution reaction took place.<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0073"/> The final charge product was partially 1T‐MoS<sub>2</sub>, and no 2H‐MoS<sub>2</sub> was found in the charging state. In this voltage range, the <i>x</i> value in the 1T‐Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>MoS<sub>2</sub> should be less than 1.5.<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0074"/> In terms of testing in 0.01–3.0 V, the platform at 0.06 V corresponded to the formation of metal Mo nanoparticles and sodium sulfide, and no MoS<sub>2</sub> could be reversibly generated in the next charging process. The possible reason may be the same as that for LIBs. In the charging process, Mo nanoparticles will remain electrochemically inert, and the alkali sulfides will be oxidized to S. Thus, the reaction in the following cycles is similar to that in the lithium–sulfur batteries. According to the DFT calculations, the calculated formation energy (<i>E</i><sub>f</sub>) suggested that the 2H‐MoS<sub>2</sub> was transformed to 1T‐MoS<sub>2</sub> when the sodium concentration was greater than 0.39 per MoS<sub>2</sub>, which was very close to the <i>x</i> value obtained from experimental results.<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0042 #adma201700606-bib-0075 #adma201700606-bib-0076"/> The <i>E</i><sub>f</sub> of Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>MoS<sub>2</sub> was higher than −1.0 eV, but the <i>E</i><sub>f</sub> of the fully discharged state is significantly lower and had the value of −15.46 eV, which indicated that the Mo metal and sodium sulfide were very stable and not easy to reverse back to MoS<sub>2</sub>. Some reports, however, assigned the platform around 1.8 V to the oxidation reaction to reform the MoS<sub>2</sub>.<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0077 #adma201700606-bib-0078 #adma201700606-bib-0079"/> Further investigations on the mechanism of the reaction are needed to provide more detailed and reasonable evidence <displayedItem type="mathematics" numbered="yes" xml:id="adma201700606-disp-0003"><mediaResource href="graphic/adma201700606-math-0003.png" alt="urn:x-wiley:dummy:media:adma201700606:adma201700606-math-0003"/></displayedItem><displayedItem type="mathematics" numbered="yes" xml:id="adma201700606-disp-0004"><mediaResource href="graphic/adma201700606-math-0004.png" alt="urn:x-wiley:dummy:media:adma201700606:adma201700606-math-0004"/></displayedItem><displayedItem type="mathematics" numbered="yes" xml:id="adma201700606-disp-0005"><mediaResource href="graphic/adma201700606-math-0005.png" alt="urn:x-wiley:dummy:media:adma201700606:adma201700606-math-0005"/></displayedItem></p><figure xml:id="adma201700606-fig-0003"><mediaResourceGroup><mediaResource href="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma201700606:adma201700606-fig-0003" alt="image"/><mediaResource mimeType="image/png" href="graphic/adma201700606-fig-0003-m.png" rendition="webHiRes" alt="image"/></mediaResourceGroup><caption>The crystal structures of a) 2H‐MoS<sub>2</sub>, b) 1T‐MoS<sub>2</sub>, and c) 3R‐MoS<sub>2</sub>. d) In situ XRD characterization of commercial MoS<sub>2</sub>/Na battery with a loading mass of 23.0 mg cm<sup>−2</sup>. The corresponding charge–discharge curve and an enlargement of the indicated region are shown on the right. e–h) High‐angle annular dark‐field (HAADF) images of commercial MoS<sub>2</sub> electrode material along the [001] zone axis: the images correspond to the cut‐off discharge capacity of 60, 80, 160, and 256 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. Annular bright‐field images of nanosized MoS<sub>2</sub> along the<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0100"/> zone axis corresponding to the i) as‐prepared MoS<sub>2</sub> sample and the cut‐off voltages of j) 1.0, k) 0.8, and l) 0.2 V. The yellow, purple, and blue circles represent the sulfur, molybdenum, and sodium atoms. d–l) Reproduced with permission.<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0042"/> Copyright 2014, American Chemical Society.</caption></figure><p>MoS<sub>2</sub> electrodes have a severe cycling problem and large voltage polarization, due to the irreversible reaction and the shuttle effects of polysulfides.<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0080 #adma201700606-bib-0081"/> Effective ways to solve these problems have been widely proposed. Nanostructure design can shorten the diffusion paths of sodium ions and provide the capability to accommodate large volume changes.<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0082 #adma201700606-bib-0083 #adma201700606-bib-0084 #adma201700606-bib-0085 #adma201700606-bib-0086 #adma201700606-bib-0087 #adma201700606-bib-0088 #adma201700606-bib-0089"/> Carbon analogues can help to confine the bad effects of volume changes, prevent the dissolution of active materials, and provide high electrical conductivity.<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0071 #adma201700606-bib-0090 #adma201700606-bib-0091 #adma201700606-bib-0092 #adma201700606-bib-0093 #adma201700606-bib-0094 #adma201700606-bib-0095"/> Reports on 1D to 3D‐structured MoS<sub>2</sub> composites are summarized in <b>Figures</b> <link href="#adma201700606-fig-0004"><b>4</b></link>–<link href="#adma201700606-fig-0007"><b>7</b></link>. Zhu et al. synthesized single‐layered MoS<sub>2</sub> nanoplates embedded in carbon nanofibers by a facile electrospinning method and calcination (Figure <link href="#adma201700606-fig-0004"/>a–e).<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0096"/> The ultrasmall particle size ensured a short Na<sup>+</sup>‐ion diffusion time, and the carbon matrix provided high electrical conductivity. Thus, the composites showed a high discharge capacity of ≈400 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 10 A g<sup>−1</sup>. Shi et al. synthesized 1D MoS<sub>2</sub>/C nanotubes through a solvothermal reaction (Figure <link href="#adma201700606-fig-0004"/>f–i).<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0097"/> The obtained material showed a capacity of 471 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>, and the capacity retention was about 100% after 200 cycles. In the meantime, the capacity could reach 187 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at a high current density of 20 A g<sup>−1</sup>. David et al. prepared a 2D MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheet/graphene paper through a liquid solution reaction, ultrasonic treatment, and subsequent calcination (Figure <link href="#adma201700606-fig-0005"/>a).<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0098"/> The improved electrochemical reaction is shown in Figure <link href="#adma201700606-fig-0005"/>b. Re‐doped 3D fullerene‐like MoS<sub>2</sub> was reported by Tenne and co‐workers (Figure <link href="#adma201700606-fig-0005"/>c–e).<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0099"/> The rate performance was optimized by Re‐doping, and a capacity of 74 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 4 A g<sup>−1</sup> with a terminal voltage at 0.7 V was obtained. Chen and co‐workers synthesized 3D MoS<sub>2</sub>/C microspheres by using a spray pyrolysis method, and the results showed an ultralong‐term cycling life with a capacity of 400 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 1 A g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <link href="#adma201700606-fig-0006"/>a–d).<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0070"/> The successful design of MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets homogeneously dispersed inside the carbon matrix provided great stability to the active materials. The microsphere structure could still be maintained after 100 cycles. Park et al. obtained hierarchical few‐layer MoS<sub>2</sub> embedded in hierarchical porous carbon as a long‐cycle‐life electrode for SIBs.<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0100"/> The cycling performance showed capacity retention of ≈70% after 300 cycles as shown in Figure <link href="#adma201700606-fig-0006"/>e–g.</p><figure xml:id="adma201700606-fig-0004"><mediaResourceGroup><mediaResource href="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma201700606:adma201700606-fig-0004" alt="image"/><mediaResource mimeType="image/png" href="graphic/adma201700606-fig-0004-m.png" rendition="webHiRes" alt="image"/></mediaResourceGroup><caption>a) TEM image, b–d) high‐resolution TEM (HRTEM) images, and e) the rate capability of single‐layered MoS<sub>2</sub> nanoplates embedded in carbon nanofibers. a–e) Reproduced with permission.<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0096"/> Copyright 2014, Wiley‐VCH. f) Scanning electron microscopy (SEM) image, g,h) HRTEM images, and i) the rate and cycling performances of MoS<sub>2</sub>/C nanotubes. f–i) Reproduced with permission.<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0097"/> Copyright 2016, Elsevier B.V.</caption></figure><figure xml:id="adma201700606-fig-0005"><mediaResourceGroup><mediaResource href="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma201700606:adma201700606-fig-0005" alt="image"/><mediaResource mimeType="image/png" href="graphic/adma201700606-fig-0005-m.png" rendition="webHiRes" alt="image"/></mediaResourceGroup><caption>a) TEM image of superacid treated MoS<sub>2</sub>, and b) rate and cycling performances of MoS<sub>2</sub>/graphene. a,b) Reproduced with permission.<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0098"/> Copyright 2014, American Chemical Society. c) TEM image, d) SEM images, and e) the rate performance of Re‐doped inorganic fullerene MoS<sub>2</sub>. c–e) Reproduced with permission.<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0099"/> Copyright 2015, Wiley‐VCH.</caption></figure><figure xml:id="adma201700606-fig-0006"><mediaResourceGroup><mediaResource href="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma201700606:adma201700606-fig-0006" alt="image"/><mediaResource mimeType="image/png" href="graphic/adma201700606-fig-0006-m.png" rendition="webHiRes" alt="image"/></mediaResourceGroup><caption>a) TEM image, b) galvanostatic curves in selected cycles, c) rate capability, and d) long‐term cycling performance of MoS<sub>2</sub>/C microspheres. a–d) Reproduced with permission.<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0070"/> Copyright 2015, Wiley‐VCH. e,f) TEM images, and g) the cycling performance of MoS<sub>2</sub>/porous carbon. e–g) Reproduced with permission.<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0100"/> Copyright 2016, American Chemical Society.</caption></figure><p>The performances were effectively enhanced through nanocrystallization and carbon modification, but the capacity retention still cannot be maintained above 90% after hundreds of cycles. Further investigations on the cut‐off voltage and electrolyte selection are responsible for the great progress on MoS<sub>2</sub>/Na batteries. According to the DFT calculations, the very stable discharging products of Mo and Na<sub>2</sub>S make the reaction hard to reverse.<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0075"/> Therefore, it is possible to avoid the conversion by cutting off the terminal voltage. Moreover, modifying the MoS<sub>2</sub> interlayers through synthetic procedures has been investigated, such as by introducing expanded layers and poly(ethylene oxide) (PEO) or graphene intercalated layers.<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0101 #adma201700606-bib-0102"/> Li et al. obtained interlayer‐expanded MoS<sub>2</sub>/PEO nanocomposites. The synthesis method and accompanying transmission electron microscopy (TEM) image are shown in Figure <link href="#adma201700606-fig-0007"/>a,b, respectively.<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0103"/> After introducing two PEO layers into adjacent S–Mo–S layers, the optimized MoS<sub>2L</sub>/PEO nanocomposites showed a capacity of 225 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 50 mA g<sup>−1</sup> after the first 5 cycles of activation. Bang et al. prepared few‐layer MoS<sub>2</sub> by liquid‐phase exfoliation.<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0061"/> The results showed highly stable charge capacities of 164 and 161 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> for the 1st and the 50th cycles at 20 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. Chen and co‐workers investigated the changes in the MoS<sub>2</sub> layers when the batteries were cycled at 0.4–3.0 V with selected 1 <sc>m</sc> NaCF<sub>3</sub>SO<sub>3</sub>/diglyme electrolyte.<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0056"/> Capacities of 200 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 1 A g<sup>−1</sup> and 175 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 10 A g<sup>−1</sup> were obtained in the first few cycles, as shown in Figure <link href="#adma201700606-fig-0007"/>c, and then the capacities were slightly increased. After cycling for 300 cycles, the capacity was increased to 350 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 50 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, which was caused by the exfoliated layers. The fewer the number of layers in the MoS<sub>2</sub> sheets, the greater the number of Na<sup>+</sup> ions that they can take up. The ex situ TEM images from the 10th, 500th, and 1000th cycles confirmed that the MoS<sub>2</sub> layers had gradually expanded and been exfoliated during cycling (Figure <link href="#adma201700606-fig-0007"/>d–f). Meanwhile, the pseudocapacitance found in the battery system facilitated long‐term cycling performance and high rate capability.</p><figure xml:id="adma201700606-fig-0007"><mediaResourceGroup><mediaResource href="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma201700606:adma201700606-fig-0007" alt="image"/><mediaResource mimeType="image/png" href="graphic/adma201700606-fig-0007-m.png" rendition="webHiRes" alt="image"/></mediaResourceGroup><caption>Schematic illustration of materials preparation of a) PEO‐intercalated MoS<sub>2</sub> composite and b) its corresponding HRTEM image. a,b) Reproduced with permission.<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0103"/> Copyright 2015, Elsevier B.V. c) Rate and cycling performance of expanded MoS<sub>2</sub>/Na battery. d–f) HRTEM images of MoS<sub>2</sub> electrode at the 10th, 500th, and 1000th cycles. c–f) Reproduced with permission.<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0056"/> Copyright 2014, Wiley‐VCH.</caption></figure></section><section xml:id="adma201700606-sec-0160"><label role="interim">3.1.2</label><title type="main">MoSe<sub>2</sub></title><p>MoSe<sub>2</sub> has a similar structure to MoS<sub>2</sub>. 2H‐MoSe<sub>2</sub> is stable at room temperature and can be obtained through common synthesis methods such as solvothermal reaction, the reflux method, and spray pyrolysis. According to previous reports, the reaction mechanism has not been clearly determined. In the initial discharge process, some reports declared that two reduction peaks found at around 0.6 and 0.3 V corresponded to the sodium intercalation into the MoSe<sub>2</sub> crystal structure and the conversion to Mo and sodium selenides, respectively.<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0104 #adma201700606-bib-0105 #adma201700606-bib-0106 #adma201700606-bib-0107 #adma201700606-bib-0108"/> Other studies, however, found a much higher reduction peak above 1.3 V, which was assigned to the sodium intercalation reaction.<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0109 #adma201700606-bib-0110 #adma201700606-bib-0111 #adma201700606-bib-0112 #adma201700606-bib-0113"/> In the oxidation process, a peak around 1.8 V was observed in most reports, but different theories have been proposed. One was that the peak corresponded to the formation of selenium.<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0106 #adma201700606-bib-0111"/> The other theory was that the peak reflected the formation of MoSe<sub>2</sub>.<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0104 #adma201700606-bib-0108"/> Some evidence such as ex situ XRD has been provided, but further investigations such as in situ XRD and TEM analysis are still needed. Due to the poor electrochemical performance of bare MoSe<sub>2</sub>, effective ways to modify bare MoSe<sub>2</sub>, such as carbon coating, porous structure design, and nanocrystallization, have been reported.<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0110 #adma201700606-bib-0114 #adma201700606-bib-0115"/> Xie et al. synthesized MoSe<sub>2</sub>/reduced graphene oxide (rGO) composites through a hydrothermal process.<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0106"/> The MoSe<sub>2</sub>/rGO composites showed improved cycling performance. The capacities at the 10th and the 100th cycles were 462 and 456 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 200 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. Tang et al. used an oleic acid assisted reflux method to synthesize MoSe<sub>2</sub>/C composite.<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0108"/> The amorphous carbon coating on the MoSe<sub>2</sub> surface stabilized the structure. Thus, the composite showed a reversible capacity of 445 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 1 A g<sup>−1</sup> over 100 cycles. Ko et al. synthesized yolk–shell MoSe<sub>2</sub> by using the selenization method to treat MoO<sub>3</sub>, as shown in <b>Figure</b> <link href="#adma201700606-fig-0008"><b>8</b></link>a,b.<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0110"/> The charge and discharge curves indicated a phase change in the initial two cycles. The rate performance was also improved, and a capacity of 345 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> was achieved at 1.5 A g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <link href="#adma201700606-fig-0008"/>c).</p><figure xml:id="adma201700606-fig-0008"><mediaResourceGroup><mediaResource href="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma201700606:adma201700606-fig-0008" alt="image"/><mediaResource mimeType="image/png" href="graphic/adma201700606-fig-0008-m.png" rendition="webHiRes" alt="image"/></mediaResourceGroup><caption>a) Schematic illustration of the yolk–shell‐structured MoSe<sub>2</sub>. b) SEM image (inset: higher magnification) and c) rate performance of as‐prepared MoSe<sub>2</sub> compared with MoO<sub>3</sub>. a–c) Reproduced with permission.<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0110"/> Copyright 2014, the Royal Society of Chemistry. d) Schematic illustration of the synthesis of the WS<sub>2</sub>/3D‐graphene composites. e) TEM image and f) the rate performance of WS<sub>2</sub>/3D‐graphene composites. d–f) Reproduced with permission.<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0123"/> Copyright 2015, Royal Society of Chemistry.</caption></figure></section><section xml:id="adma201700606-sec-0170"><label role="interim">3.1.3</label><title type="main">WS<sub>2</sub> and WSe<sub>2</sub></title><p>Tungsten and molybdenum are both group 6 transition metals, and they share similar structures and properties. WS<sub>2</sub> and WSe<sub>2</sub> belong to the hexagonal phase. The mechanisms of WS<sub>2</sub> and WSe<sub>2</sub> include both the sodium‐ion‐intercalation process and the conversion reaction during the initial discharge process.<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0116 #adma201700606-bib-0117 #adma201700606-bib-0118"/> Suffering from the common failings of MXs, the cycling performances of bare WS<sub>2</sub> and WSe<sub>2</sub> are not satisfactory.<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0119"/> To improve the cycling performance, Su et al. prepared WS<sub>2</sub>/graphene composites, which showed a capacity of ≈600 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 20 mA g<sup>−1</sup>.<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0120"/> Chen and co‐workers prepared WS<sub>2</sub> nanowires through a solvothermal reaction and subsequent calcination in Ar atmosphere.<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0121"/> The obtained WS<sub>2</sub> nanowires exhibited capacities of 550 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup> and 236 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 5 A g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. Wang et al. prepared free‐standing WS<sub>2</sub>/carbon nanotube (CNT)‐rGO aerogel by applying an ice template method.<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0122"/> The resultant product displayed a capacity of 311 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup>. Choi et al. obtained WS<sub>2</sub>/3D‐graphene composites by using spray pyrolysis and a sulfuration process (Figure <link href="#adma201700606-fig-0008"/>d).<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0123"/> The void space of 3D‐graphene derived from the decomposition of polystyrene (PS) template was employed to accommodate the volume changes during cycling (Figure <link href="#adma201700606-fig-0008"/>e). Thus, the WS<sub>2</sub>/3D‐graphene showed greatly improved stability compared to the sample without PS template, and the rate capability was enhanced by the 3D‐graphene conductive networks (Figure <link href="#adma201700606-fig-0008"/>f). WSe<sub>2</sub> was also synthesized through solid‐state reaction by using tungsten powder, selenium, and Super P carbon.<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0118"/> The initial capacity was 300 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>, and then it dropped to 270 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at the 50th cycle at 200 mA g<sup>−1</sup>.</p></section></section><section xml:id="adma201700606-sec-0180"><label role="interim">3.2</label><title type="main">SnS<sub>2</sub> and SnS</title><p>SnS<sub>2</sub> and SnS have received tremendous attention due to their high theoretical capacities of 1022 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> and 1136 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. The mechanism of their reactions is summarized in Equations <link href="#adma201700606-disp-0006"/> and <link href="#adma201700606-disp-0007"/>. After the conversion reaction in the discharge process, tin can alloy with sodium to form Na<sub>3.75</sub>Sn, which leads to an ultrahigh theoretical capacity.<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0124 #adma201700606-bib-0125 #adma201700606-bib-0126 #adma201700606-bib-0127 #adma201700606-bib-0128"/> SnS<sub>2</sub> has a CdI<sub>2</sub>‐type layered structure with an interlayer spacing of 0.59 nm. Normally, researchers prefer to pursue improved electrochemical properties by integrating SnS<sub>2</sub> with graphene or other carbon materials.<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0129 #adma201700606-bib-0130 #adma201700606-bib-0131 #adma201700606-bib-0132"/> For example, Qu et al. prepared SnS<sub>2</sub>/rGO composite through a hydrothermal synthesis.<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0133"/> The as‐prepared SnS<sub>2</sub>/rGO anode delivered a high specific capacity of 500 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> after 400 cycles at 1 A g<sup>−1</sup>, and good rate performance of 544 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 2 A g<sup>−1</sup>. The binder‐free SnS<sub>2</sub>/rGO nanosheet arrays on carbon cloth prepared by Xu et al. also exhibited good cycling performance.<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0134"/> A capacity of 378 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> was maintained at 1.2 A g<sup>−1</sup> after 200 cycles. The discharge capacities at specific currents of 0.2, 1, 2, and 3 A g<sup>−1</sup> were 633, 523, 458, and 348 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively <displayedItem type="mathematics" numbered="yes" xml:id="adma201700606-disp-0006"><mediaResource href="graphic/adma201700606-math-0006.png" alt="urn:x-wiley:dummy:media:adma201700606:adma201700606-math-0006"/></displayedItem><displayedItem type="mathematics" numbered="yes" xml:id="adma201700606-disp-0007"><mediaResource href="graphic/adma201700606-math-0007.png" alt="urn:x-wiley:dummy:media:adma201700606:adma201700606-math-0007"/></displayedItem></p><p>SnS features a distorted NaCl‐type structure. Hexagonal‐SnS<sub>2</sub> was transformed into orthorhombic‐SnS by Zhou et al. through an annealing step in an argon atmosphere.<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0135"/> The authors concluded that the SnS experienced the structural transformation from orthorhombic‐SnS to cubic‐Sn to orthorhombic‐Na<sub>3.75</sub>Sn, while the SnS<sub>2</sub> possessed the mechanism from hexagonal‐SnS<sub>2</sub> to tetragonal‐Sn to orthorhombic‐Na<sub>3.75</sub>Sn during the cycling process. They also argued that the SnS phase was reproduced during desodiation, and less structural change provided good structural stability and excellent cycling stability for sodium‐ion storage in SnS. The SnS/graphene composite delivered a specific capacity of 492 and 308 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at the current densities of 810 and 7290 mA g<sup>−1</sup> after 250 cycles, respectively. Carbon modification of SnS has also received great attention.<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0136 #adma201700606-bib-0137 #adma201700606-bib-0138 #adma201700606-bib-0139"/> SnS/C composite powders were prepared by Choi et al. through a spray pyrolysis method.<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0140"/> The discharge capacity of the as‐prepared SnS/C composite was 433 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> after 50 cycles at 500 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, and the capacity retention was 89% compared with the second cycle. Recently, graphene‐foam‐supported SnS nanoparticles were reported for SIBs.<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0054"/> The three kinds of morphology shown in <b>Figure</b> <link href="#adma201700606-fig-0009"><b>9</b></link>a displayed great improvement in their cycling and rate performances (Figure <link href="#adma201700606-fig-0009"/>b,c). Among them, the nanohoneycomb SnS exhibited high reversible capacities of ≈1100 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 30 mA g<sup>−1</sup> and ≈420 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 30 A g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. The excellent high‐capacity and high‐rate sodium‐ion storage performance was attributed to its maximized extrinsic pseudocapacitance contribution (Figure <link href="#adma201700606-fig-0009"/>d).</p><figure xml:id="adma201700606-fig-0009"><mediaResourceGroup><mediaResource href="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma201700606:adma201700606-fig-0009" alt="image"/><mediaResource mimeType="image/png" href="graphic/adma201700606-fig-0009-m.png" rendition="webHiRes" alt="image"/></mediaResourceGroup><caption>a) SEM images of SnS samples with different morphologies. b) Cycling and c) rate performances of the three kinds of as‐prepared samples. d) CV curves at 0.8 mV s<sup>−1</sup> (left) and pseudocapacitance contribution analysis of the nanohoneycomb sample (right). Reproduced with permission.<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0054"/> Copyright 2016, Nature Publishing Group.</caption></figure></section><section xml:id="adma201700606-sec-0190"><label role="interim">3.3</label><title type="main">TiS<sub>2</sub></title><p>TiS<sub>2</sub> has been widely investigated in relation to Li chemistry since the 1970s.<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0031"/> Although it features good reversibility for lithium intercalation and deintercalation, TiS<sub>2</sub> is not used in commercial applications due to the safety issues. Recently, some reports applied TiS<sub>2</sub> as an electrode for SIBs with long‐term stability.<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0141 #adma201700606-bib-0142 #adma201700606-bib-0143 #adma201700606-bib-0144"/> The synthesized room temperature product TiS<sub>2</sub> belongs to the <i>P</i><inlineGraphic href="graphic/adma201700606-math-0008.png" alt="urn:x-wiley:dummy:media:adma201700606:adma201700606-math-0008"/><i>m</i>1 trigonal phase, and the common interlayer spacing is 0.57 nm. TiS<sub>2</sub> exhibited an intercalation reaction when the terminal voltage was set to 1.0 V.<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0144"/> According to the Na<sup>+</sup> concentration in the Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>TiS<sub>2</sub>, several <i>x</i> ranges existed. When the <i>x</i> value was lower than 0.5, the interlayers gradually expanded as sodium intercalated into the TiS<sub>2</sub> structure.<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0145"/> The interlayer spacing, however, decreased as the <i>x</i> value was further increased. The reaction was reversible in the charging process, and TiS<sub>2</sub> was finally formed. Liu et al. prepared TiS<sub>2</sub> nanoplates by reflux reaction and calcination under Ar atmosphere.<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0144"/> The obtained TiS<sub>2</sub> nanoplates showed a capacity of 186 and 101 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 24 and 2400 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. The long‐term cycling performance at 480 mA g<sup>−1</sup> featured a capacity of about 175 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at the 2nd cycle and a capacity of about 140 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at the 300th cycle.</p></section><section xml:id="adma201700606-sec-0200"><label role="interim">3.4</label><title type="main">VS<sub>2</sub>, VS<sub>4</sub>, and NbS<sub>2</sub></title><p>V and Nb are both group 5 transition metals, and their disulfide compounds, VS<sub>2</sub> and NbS<sub>2</sub>, exhibit metallic conductivity, which is very important for achieving high rate capability. There are only a few reports, however, that focus on these materials.<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0146 #adma201700606-bib-0147 #adma201700606-bib-0148 #adma201700606-bib-0149 #adma201700606-bib-0150"/> Mai and co‐workers obtained VS<sub>4</sub>/rGO composites.<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0151"/> The results showed that the VS<sub>4</sub> cannot be reproduced in the charge process, which resulted in bad cycling performance. Ou et al. prepared NbS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets by a solid state sulfuration method and proved that the reaction mechanism was quite different from that of other TMDs.<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0147"/> In the voltage range of 0.01–3.0 V, no obvious conversion reaction was found according to the in situ XRD characterization, and the final product was Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>NbS<sub>2</sub>. The electrochemical tests showed a discharge capacity of ≈190 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup>, and the capacity remained about 157 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> after 100 cycles.</p></section></section><section xml:id="adma201700606-sec-0210"><label role="interim">4</label><title type="main">Nonlayered MXs</title><p>Non‐layered MXs (M = Fe, Co, Ni, Cu, Zn, Sb, Bi and X = S, Se) are also very popular. Some of them can be obtained from natural minerals, such as chalcopyrite, pyrite, marcasite, and sphalerite. Thus, the advantages of low price and high theoretical capacity make them very competitive compared to other anode materials.</p><section xml:id="adma201700606-sec-0220"><label role="interim">4.1</label><title type="main">Iron‐Based MXs</title><section xml:id="adma201700606-sec-0230"><label role="interim">4.1.1</label><title type="main">Pyrite FeS<sub>2</sub></title><p>The pyrite FeS<sub>2</sub> is a natural mineral, which is widely used to obtain sulfuric acid. Its low price makes it very competitive as a commercial electrode material for battery devices. As early as the 1990s, Energizer Corporation first produced commercial primary FeS<sub>2</sub>/Li 18650‐type batteries. With the strong demand for rechargeable use, the cycling performance is one of the most important issues for FeS<sub>2</sub> for use in SIBs. FeS<sub>2</sub> features a cubic structure and belongs to the <i>Pa</i><inlineGraphic href="graphic/adma201700606-math-0009.png" alt="urn:x-wiley:dummy:media:adma201700606:adma201700606-math-0009"/> space group, in which Fe occupies the (FeS<sub>6</sub>) octahedral sites and sulfur atoms exist as S<sub>2</sub><sup>2−</sup>. During the discharge process, S<sub>2</sub><sup>2−</sup> is first reduced to S<sup>2−</sup>, and then Fe<sup>2+</sup> is transformed to Fe along with the generation of sodium sulfides.<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0055"/> Although, there are different theories about the voltage platforms, which have been summarized in <b>Table</b> <link href="#adma201700606-tbl-0002"><b>2</b></link>,<link href="#adma201700606-bib-0152 #adma201700606-bib-0153 #adma201700606-bib-0154"/> the main difference is whether Na<sub>2</sub>S and Fe can be formed above 0.8 V or not. In the charge process, a new charge product, Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>FeS<sub>2</sub>, is generated instead of the original pyrite FeS<sub>2</sub>. The whole reaction provides a high theoretical specific capacity of 894 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>.</p><tabular xml:id="adma201700606-tbl-0002" pRights="unknown" eRights="yes" copyright="John Wiley &amp; href="#adma201700606-bib-0059"/></entry></row><row><entry colname="col1" align="left">FeS<sub>2</sub> microspheres</entry><entry colname="col2" align="center">Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>FeS<sub>2</sub> (<i>x</i> &lt; 2)</entry><entry colname="col3" align="center">0.8</entry><entry colname="col4" align="center">Ex situ XRD, XANES, ex situ TEM</entry><entry colname="col5" align="center"><link href="#adma201700606-bib-0055"/></entry></row><row><entry colname="col1" align="left"/><entry colname="col2" align="center">FeS</entry><entry colname="col3" align="center">0.5</entry><entry colname="col4" align="center"/><entry colname="col5" align="center"/></row><row><entry colname="col1" align="left"/><entry colname="col2" align="center">Na<sub>2</sub>S and Fe</entry><entry colname="col3" align="center">0.1</entry><entry colname="col4" align="center"/><entry colname="col5" align="center"/></row><row><entry colname="col1"
